Defining Zaahir and Khafi in Hanafi Usul
Defining zaahir and khafi with Quranic examples and oath example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between zaahir and khafi and apply the ruling that khafi requires seeking clarification.

Core Idea
A text clear in general but hidden in some applications requires active investigation.

In this lesson, we explore two foundational categories of textual clarity in Hanafi usul: al-zaahir (the apparent) and al-khafi (the hidden). The text shows how a single term can be clear for typical cases but ambiguous for edge cases. The key ruling is that khafi demands seeking (talab) to resolve the ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms
Note the two Arabic terms: al-zaahir and al-khafi. The passage defines them through examples.
2
Analyze the first example
The Quranic verse on theft is zaahir for the common thief (saariq) but khafi for the pickpocket (tarrar) and grave-robber (nabbaash). Why? Because those are not the usual meaning of 'saariq'.
Think about the distinction between typical and atypical applications.
3
Analyze the second example
The verse on fornication is zaahir for the fornicator (zaani) but khafi for the sodomite (looti). The term 'zaani' typically means male-female fornication, so it is hidden for sodomy.
4
Analyze the oath example
If someone swears not to eat 'fruit' (faakiha), it is zaahir for common fruits (like dates) but khafi for grapes and pomegranates. The common understanding of 'fruit' may not include these.
This shows how context affects clarity.
5
Extract the ruling
The passage states: 'حكم الخفي وجوب الطلب' — it is obligatory to seek clarification for khafi until the hiddenness is removed. This means one must investigate the intended meaning.
This is the legal consequence: talab is required, not ta'ammul (deep reflection).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المتقابلات في قوله تعالى: وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا [المائدة: ۳۸] فإنّه ﴿ ﴾ کیسه بُر. ۱۲ کفن کش. ۱۲ ظاهر في حق السارق خفي في حق الطرار والنبّاش، وكذلك قوله تعالى: أي مثل قوله تعالى و السارق... إلخ. ۱۲ الزَّانِيَةُ وَالزَّانِي [النور: ۲] ظاهر في حق الزاني خفي في حق اللوطي، ولو وهو من عمل قوم لوط. ١٢ قوله لا يأكل فاكهة. ١٢ حلف لا يأكل فاكهة كان ظاهرا فيما يتفكه به خفيًّا في حق العنب والرّمّان، انگور: ۱۲ انار. ۱۲ حكم الخفي وجوب الطلب حتى يزول عنه الخفاء. إشارة إلى وجه التسمية. ١٢
A chapter on contrasting terms. With regard to the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And the male thief and the female thief, cut off their hands' [al-Ma'idah 5:38], it is zaahir with respect to the thief, khafi with respect to the pickpocket and the grave-robber. Similarly, the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'The female fornicator and the male fornicator' [al-Nur 24:2] is zaahir with respect to the fornicator, khafi with respect to the sodomite. And if someone swears an oath not to eat fruit, it is zaahir with respect to what is normally eaten as fruit, khafi with respect to grapes and pomegranates. The ruling for the khafi is that it is obligatory to seek (clarification) until the hiddenness is removed. An indication of the reason for the naming.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage introduces two levels of textual clarity: zaahir (apparent) and khafi (hidden). The author begins by stating that the Quranic verse about theft is zaahir (clear) in its application to the common thief, but it is khafi (hidden) in its application to the pickpocket and the grave-robber because these are not the typical cases. Similarly, the verse about fornication is zaahir for the fornicator but khafi for the sodomite. The third example is an oath: if someone swears not to eat fruit, it is zaahir that it includes common fruits like dates, but khafi whether it includes grapes and pomegranates. The ruling for khafi is that one must seek clarification (طلب) until the hiddenness is removed, i.e., one must investigate the intended meaning. The commentary notes that the term 'khafi' is named because it is hidden in some individuals.

Key Terms
الظاهر (al-zaahir): ما ظهر للسامع خفاء في نفسه لكنه خفي في حق بعض الأفراد
الخفي (al-khafi): ما خفي على السامع في بعض الأفراد دون بعض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for the khafi (hidden) text?
A) It is to be ignored.
B) It is obligatory to seek clarification until the hiddenness is removed.
C) It requires deep reflection.
D) It is clear without investigation.

Key Takeaway
The hiddenness of a text in some applications does not invalidate it; it demands active investigation to resolve the ambiguity.

Abandoning Literal Meanings Due to Custom: Haqiqa Qasira
Introduces the first factor for abandoning literal meanings: linguistic custom, with examples and the concept of haqiqa qasira.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of linguistic custom ('urf qawli) in restricting literal meanings, and distinguish haqiqa qasira from majaz in the Hanafi usul framework.

Core Idea
Custom restricts literal meaning without shifting to metaphor—haqiqa qasira bridges the gap.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi introduces the first of five factors that cause a word to depart from its literal meaning: customary usage. The author emphasizes that linguistic custom, not practical custom, governs the interpretation of words in legal contexts. Importantly, he establishes that when a literal meaning is abandoned, it does not necessarily become metaphorical; instead, an intermediate category—haqiqa qasira (incomplete literal meaning)—may apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five causes
The passage states there are five things that cause abandonment of haqiqa; the first is custom. Note that this is derived by induction (istiqra).
The text says 'عرف بالاستقراء' – known by induction.
2
Understand the role of custom
Custom here is linguistic custom ('urf fi isti'mal al-alfaz), not practical custom ('urf amali). The commentary clarifies that only linguistic custom is considered in interpreting words.
Practical custom is not legally binding in Hanafi usul for this purpose.
3
Analyze the examples
Two examples: oath not to buy 'ra's' (head) and oath not to eat 'bayd' (eggs). The custom restricts the literal meaning to specific types (roasted sheep head, chicken eggs). The oath is not broken by buying a bird's head or eating sparrow eggs.
Abu Hanifa specified 'ra's' as the head of a cow or sheep roasted and sold in markets.
4
Distinguish haqiqa qasira from majaz
The passage argues that abandoning the broad literal meaning does not entail metaphor. Instead, the word is used in a haqiqa qasira (incomplete literal meaning) – still literal but confined to the customary denotation.
Haqiqa qasira is intermediate between haqiqa kamilah (complete) and majaz.
5
Apply the principle
In legal rulings, the customary meaning is the default. When a word is restricted by custom, it creates a new literal meaning for that context, and the legal effect (e.g., ruling of oath) is based on that restricted meaning.
This principle is derived from the two examples and applies to other similar cases.

Chapter on what causes the literal meanings to be abandoned. The literal meaning of a word is abandoned due to five things, known by induction. (1) One type: indication of custom (urf). This is because the establishment of rulings through words is for the word. (2) Such that it comes to mind upon hearing it. (3) Because the word indicates the meaning intended by the speaker. So when the meaning is customary among people, that customary meaning becomes evidence that it is the apparent intended meaning, and the abandonment of the literal meaning occurs due to custom. Example: If someone swears that he will not buy a 'head' (ra's), then it is interpreted according to what people customarily mean by 'head', so he does not break his oath by buying a head of a sparrow or pigeon, unless he intends otherwise. Similarly, if he swears that he will not eat 'eggs' (bayd), then it is interpreted according to customary eggs, so he does not break his oath by eating eggs of sparrow or pigeon. And from this it appears that abandoning the literal meaning does not necessitate resorting to metaphor; rather, it is possible that an incomplete literal meaning (haqiqa qasira) is established. E.g., restricting the general to a part. (1) His statement: [This is a beginning of explaining the abandonment of literal meanings... abandoning literal meanings does not necessitate intending metaphor; rather, it is permissible to intend the incomplete literal meaning.] (2) His statement: [Indication of custom] and what is meant by custom is custom in the use of words and understanding meanings from them, not custom in terms of transactions, because it is known that practical custom is not considered. Thus in 'al-Ma'din'. (3) His statement: It is according to what people customarily mean by 'head' – and that is what is roasted in ovens and sold grilled, which is the head of cows and sheep according to Abu Hanifa. (4) His statement: [And from this it appears...] This is an exposition of a principle derived from the two transmitted examples: that when the literal meaning is abandoned, the metaphor is not necessarily intended; rather, the incomplete literal meaning may be intended, because it is intermediate between the complete literal meaning and metaphor. (5) His statement: [The incomplete literal meaning...] It is that a word is used to refer to some individuals of the literal meaning, like referring to the head of a cow or sheep from the word 'ra's', and similarly referring to chicken or goose eggs from the word 'bayd'. The word is applied to what is intended as a literal meaning, but not absolutely; because absoluteness is perfection in encompassing its denotation, so when absoluteness goes, it becomes incomplete. (6) His statement: [by the part] i.e., specifying it to a part. And from it is restricting the absolute by a restriction. So Hajj, although it is literally 'absolutely intending or heading', in the legal custom it became a complete literal meaning according to the Sharia, or an incomplete literal meaning according to language.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage introduces a crucial principle in Hanafi usul: the abandonment of the literal meaning (haqiqa) for a word can occur due to five factors; the first is customary usage (urf). Custom here refers to linguistic custom – how people commonly use words, not practical custom (which is not legally binding in Hanafi usul). The author gives two classic examples: an oath not to buy 'ra's' (head) – customarily meaning a roasted sheep's head sold in markets, so buying a bird's head doesn't break the oath unless intended. Similarly for 'bayd' (eggs) – customarily chicken eggs, so sparrow eggs don't break the oath. Importantly, the commentary clarifies that abandoning the literal meaning does not automatically mean the word is used metaphorically (majaz). Instead, it may be used in a 'haqiqa qasira' (incomplete literal meaning). This is a key Hanafi concept: there is an intermediate category between complete literal meaning and metaphor, where the word is still used literally but not for all its original denotations – it is restricted by custom to specific individuals. The footnote by the editor (possibly from Ahsan al-Hawashi) explains that custom here is linguistic custom (urf fi isti'mal al-alfaz), not practical custom (urf amali) which is not considered. The example of Hajj is briefly mentioned: it originally means 'to intend' but in legal custom it means the pilgrimage to Mecca – a complete literal meaning in Sharia but an incomplete one from a linguistic perspective. This shows how custom can transform a word's meaning without making it metaphorical.

Key Terms
عُرْف (urf): Customary usage of words (linguistic custom) that determines the intended meaning in legal contexts.
حَقِيقَة قَاصِرَة (haqiqa qasira): An incomplete literal meaning where the word is used literally but restricted by custom to certain individuals or types, serving as an intermediate between complete literal meaning and metaphor.
مَجَاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage where the word is used for something other than its original literal meaning due to a relationship (qarina).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to buy a 'ra's' and then buys a bird's head, does he break his oath according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Yes, because 'ra's' literally includes any head
B) No, because custom restricts 'ra's' to a roasted sheep's head
C) Yes, unless he specifically intended bird heads
D) No, because it is a metaphorical usage

Key Takeaway
Linguistic custom can restrict a word's literal meaning without making it metaphorical—haqiqa qasira is the Hanafi intermediate category.

Leaving Literal Meanings: Custom and Internal Indication
Explains the first two of five types of leaving literal meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two ways in which the literal meaning of a word is abandoned in Hanafi uṣūl (custom and internal indication) and apply the concept of ḥaqīqah qāṣirah to distinguish it from metaphor.

Core Idea
Leaving the literal meaning does not always require metaphor; custom or internal speech indications can establish a restricted literal meaning.

In Hanafi legal theory, words are understood according to their literal meanings unless a valid reason compels departure. This passage introduces the first two of five such reasons: customary usage (dāllat al-ʿurf) and internal evidence within the speech itself. Understanding these categories is crucial for correctly interpreting legal language without prematurely resorting to metaphor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types mentioned
The passage lists dāllat al-ʿurf and dāllah fī nafs al-kalām as the first two of five ways to leave literal meanings. Note that the text follows an inductive enumeration.
The full list is five, but only the first two are detailed here.
2
Analyze the first type: custom (ʿurf)
When a word has a customary usage, that usage determines the intended meaning, not the full literal range. Example: swearing not to buy a 'head' — custom restricts to livestock heads sold grilled.
Custom here is verbal custom (ʿurf qawlī), not practical custom.
3
Understand the concept of ḥaqīqah qāṣirah
The commentary emphasizes that abandoning the full literal meaning does not automatically lead to metaphor. Instead, it can yield a restricted literal meaning (ḥaqīqah qāṣirah), which is a subset of the original meaning.
This is unique to Hanafi uṣūl; other schools may treat such cases as metaphor.
4
Analyze the second type: internal indication
When the word itself (by its definition) contains a condition that restricts its application, the literal meaning is left. Example: 'mamlūk' implies full ownership, so it does not include a mukātab unless intended.
Intention can extend the meaning but must be explicit.
5
Apply the rule to new cases
Use the principle to interpret similar oaths or vows where custom or internal word structure limits meaning, distinguishing between full literal, restricted literal, and metaphorical uses.
Practice by constructing examples from fiqh.

(٢) قوله: [دلالة العرف] والمراد بالعرف العرف في استعمال الألفاظ وتفهم المعاني منها، لا العرف من حيث التعامل لما عرف أنّ العرف العملي لا اعتبار لها، كذا في "المعدن". (٣) قوله فهو على ما تعارفه الناس من الرأس وهو ما يكبس في التنانير ويباع مشويّاً وهو رأس البقر والغنم عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، كذا في "المعدن". (٤) قوله: [ وبهذا ظهر ... إلخ هذا بيان قاعده مستفادة من المسئلتين المنقولتين وهي أنه إذا تركت الحقيقة لا يتعيّن المجاز مراداً، بل جاز أن يراد به الحقيقة القاصرة؛ لأنها واسطة بين الحقيقة الكاملة والمجاز. (٥) قوله: [الحقيقة القاصرة ... إلخ وهي أن يراد به بعض أفراد الحقيقة كإرادة رأس البقر والغنم من لفظ «الرأس»، وكذلك إرادة بيض الدجاجة والإوز من لفظ «البيض» فإنّ اللفظ يطلق على ما أريد به حقيقة، لكنه لا مطلقاً؛ إذ الإطلاق كمال في تناول موجبه، فإذا ذهب الإطلاق كان قاصراً. (٦) قوله: [بالبعض] أي: تخصيصه به ومنه تقييد المطلق بالقيد، فالحج وإن كان حقيقة في مطلق الغلبة أو القصد لغةً فهو في العرف الشرعي صار حقيقةً كاملةً بحسب الشرع أو قاصرةً بحسب اللغة. (١) قوله: [لوجود العرف] فإنّ الناس يريدون به التزام الحجّ وإن كان معناه اللغوي لا يقتضي لزوم الحجّ، فاللفظ العامّ لَمّا أريد به الخاص فكان حقيقة قاصرة لا مجازاً، كذا في "المعدن". (٢) قوله: [لم يعتق... إلخ] لأنّ الحقيقة المتروكة في المكاتب ومعتق البعض كالمجاز فلا يصير مراداً باللفظ إلا بالنية كذا في "الفصول". (٣) قوله: [إلا إذا نوى... آه] أي: لما كانت الحقيقة وهي كلّ ما فيه ملك ولو بوجه بالقيام مبدأ الاشتقاق به مهجورة، بل أريد بالقيام وصف الكمال من دلالة إطلاق الكلام بقيام المبدء التام به لا يراد إلا المملوك الكامل هاهنا إلا إذا نوى دخول الناقص أيضاً؛ لأنها حينيذ بمنزلة المجاز أو الكناية يحتاج فيه إلى النية والقصد إلى إدخال كلّ ما فيه ملك ولو بنحو ما، وتعميم الكلية بلا لحاظ خصوص الكمال فيعتق المكاتب أيضاً، لكن لا يصح قضاءً إخراج المدبّر وأمّ الولد؛ لأنه لا دلالة للفظ عليه، وفيه تخفيف له أيضاً، وفي إدخال المكاتب تشديد على نفسه وفي اللفظ أيضاً دلالة فيعتبر قضاء أيضاً، كذا في "الفصول". (٤) قوله: [لم يجز تصرفه فيه] أي: في المكاتب في البيع والهبة، ولو كان مملوكاً من كل وجه لجاز تصرفه، ومعتق البعض كالمكاتب عند الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله. (٥) قوله: [ولو تزوّج... آه] تفريع على السابق أي: عدم كون المكاتب مملوكاً له ملكاً تاماً، فإذا تزوّج بنت مولاه ثم مات مولاه وورثت زوجها المكاتب لأبيها، وملكته بحكم الإرث لم يفسد نكاحها معه.
Section on what causes leaving the literal meanings of words. The types of leaving the literal meaning of a word are five, known by induction. One of them: indication of custom; this is because the establishment of rulings by words is only due to the indication of the word to the intended meaning of the speaker, so if the meaning is customary among people, that customary meaning becomes evidence that it is the intended meaning apparently, so its ruling follows. Example: if he swears not to buy a 'head', it is based on what people customarily mean, so he does not break the oath by buying the head of a sparrow or pigeon. Similarly, if he swears not to eat 'eggs', it is based on custom, so he does not break the oath by eating sparrow or pigeon eggs. From this it is shown that leaving the literal meaning does not necessitate moving to metaphor; rather, the restricted literal meaning (ḥaqīqah qāṣirah) can be established. Its example is restricting the general to some. Also, if he vows to perform hajj or walk to the House of God or to strike the 'Ḥaṭīm' of the Kaaba with his garment, the vow of hajj becomes binding with known actions due to custom. The second: the literal meaning may be left due to an indication in the speech itself. Example: if he says 'every slave I own is free', his mukātabūn and those who are partially freed do not become free unless he intends their inclusion; because the word 'mamlūk' (owned) is absolute and includes that which is owned in every respect, but the mukātab is not owned in every respect, and therefore he cannot dispose of him, nor is it permissible for him to have sexual intercourse with the mukātaba. And if the mukātab marries his master's daughter and then the master dies and she inherits her husband the mukātab from her father, the marriage is not invalidated. Then the footnotes: ...
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a rule statement from Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh explaining the first two of five ways in which the literal (ḥaqīqī) meaning of a word is abandoned. The first is dāllat al-ʿurf (customary indication): when a word is used, its primary literal meaning is set aside in favor of what is customary among people. For example, if someone swears not to buy a 'head' (raʾs), the custom determines that this refers to grilled heads of livestock (cattle, sheep), not any head like a sparrow's. Similarly for 'eggs'—custom refers to chicken eggs, not sparrow eggs. Crucially, the commentator emphasizes that this abandonment does not automatically lead to majāz (metaphor); rather, it establishes a ḥaqīqah qāṣirah (restricted literal meaning), which is a middle ground between full literal meaning and metaphor. The second type is dāllah fī nafs al-kalām (indication within the speech): the word itself contains evidence that its literal meaning is not fully applicable. For instance, the word 'mamlūk' (owned) in 'every mamlūk I own is free' is absolute in meaning 'fully owned', so it does not include a mukātab (slave who has made a contract to purchase freedom) because he is not fully owned. However, if the owner intends inclusion, it becomes effective by virtue of intention resorting to metaphor or allusion. The footnotes elaborate on sources: from al-Maʿdin, al-Fuṣūl, etc., and affirm that this is the position of Abu Hanifa. The passage follows a pattern: statement of principle, example, and explanation of the governing rule. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as it draws from foundational texts.

Key Terms
حقيقة قاصرة (ḥaqīqah qāṣirah): A literal meaning that is restricted to some individuals or subsets of the full literal meaning; it is between full literal meaning (ḥaqīqah kāmilah) and metaphor (majāz).
دلالة العرف (dāllat al-ʿurf): The indication of custom in the use of words and understanding meanings; it is a ground for leaving the literal meaning in favor of customary usage.
دلالة في نفس الكلام (dāllah fī nafs al-kalām): An indication within the speech itself that restricts the literal meaning, based on the internal structure or definition of the word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to buy a 'head' (raʾs), what is the Hanafi ruling if he buys a fish head?
A) He broke the oath because 'head' includes fish heads.
B) He did not break the oath because custom restricts 'head' to livestock heads sold grilled.
C) The oath is invalid due to ambiguity.
D) The ruling depends on his intention at the time of the oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi uṣūl, abandoning the literal meaning by custom or internal indication does not necessarily lead to metaphor; it can yield a restricted literal meaning (ḥaqīqah qāṣirah).

Contextual Indication Restricting General Words: The Case of 'Mamluk'
Usul rule with example from slavery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a general word ('amm) is restricted to its complete literal meaning when a contextual indication (dalala fi nafs al-kalam) points to a specific subset, and identify when a mukatab or partially free slave is not included under 'mamluk'.

Core Idea
A general word is interpreted by its complete meaning unless context indicates otherwise; incomplete instances require express intention.

In Hanafi usul, the default for a word is its literal meaning (haqiqah). However, the context of the speech itself (dalala fi nafs al-kalam) can restrict that meaning. This lesson examines the classic example of the word 'mamluk' (slave) in the statement 'Every slave of mine is free.' The context of absolute ownership leads to the understanding that only completely owned slaves are included, excluding those with incomplete ownership like mukatabs. You will learn to identify such contextual indications and apply the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general word
Find the general term in the phrase. Here, 'mamluk' is a noun that, linguistically, includes anyone who is owned, even partially.
The word is absolute (mutlaq) in form.
2
Determine the literal scope
The literal meaning (haqiqah) of 'mamluk' is 'one who is owned.' But in common usage, the absolute form implies complete ownership (mamluk min kull wajh).
This is the 'haqiqah' according to language.
3
Recognize the contextual indication
The speech 'every slave of mine is free' is absolute; there is no qualifier. In such a context, the default is to understand the word in its complete sense, excluding those with deficient ownership (mukatab, mu'taq ba'd).
The internal indication (dalala fi nafs al-kalam) is the lack of any exception or qualification.
4
Apply the rule
Thus, a mukatab or partially free slave is not freed unless the speaker expressly intends to include them. This is because the abandoned literal meaning (including incomplete ownership) is like a metaphor, requiring intentional extension.
Compare with mudabbar and umm walad, who are fully owned and therefore included without intention.

Source Text

فصل فيما يترك به حقائق الألفاظ أي ترك الحقيقة بدلالة في نفس الكلام، مثاله إذا قال: كل مملوك لي فهو حرّ لم يعتق مكاتبوه ولا من أعتق بعضه إلا إذا نوى دخولهم؛ لأن لفظ المملوك مطلق يتناول المملوك من كل وجه والمكاتب ليس بمملوك من كل وجه ولهذا لم يجز تصرفه فيه ولا يحل له وطئ المكاتبة ولو تزوّج المكاتب بنت مولاه ثم مات المولى وورثته البنت لم يفسد النكاح وإذا لم يكن مملوكاً من كل وجه لا يدخل تحت لفظ المملوك المطلق وهذا بخلاف المدبّر وأمّ الولد فإن الملك فيهما كامل ولذا حل وطئ المدبرة وأم الولد وإنّما النقصان في الرق من جهة التصرفات اللازمة. وكذلك ضرب الحطيم بثوبه وإن كان حقيقة لغة في مطلق ضربه نحو كان فقد صار في العرف العام معروفاً متعارفاً في فعل الحج، وكذلك المشي إلى بيت الله مطلق في مشيه إليها أي الكعبة، ولو بإرادة زيارتها أو للعمرة والتجارة وغيرها فهو في العرف جاء مستعملاً في إرادة الحج. (قوله: [لوجود العرف] فإنّ الناس يريدون به التزام الحجّ وإن كان معناه اللغوي لا يقتضي لزوم الحجّ، فاللفظ العامّ لَمّا أريد به الخاص فكان حقيقة قاصرة لا مجازاً، كذا في "المعدن".) (قوله: [لم يعتق... إلخ] لأنّ الحقيقة المتروكة في المكاتب ومعتق البعض كالمجاز فلا يصير مراداً باللفظ إلا بالنية كذا في "الفصول".) (قوله: [إلا إذا نوى... آه] أي: لما كانت الحقيقة وهي كلّ ما فيه ملك ولو بوجه بالقيام مبدء الاشتقاق به مهجورة، بل أريد بالقيام وصف الكمال من دلالة إطلاق الكلام بقيام المبدء التام به لا يراد إلا المملوك الكامل هاهنا إلا إذا نوى دخول الناقص أيضاً؛ لأنها حينيذ بمنزلة المجاز أو الكناية يحتاج فيه إلى النية والقصد إلى إدخال كلّ ما فيه ملك ولو بنحو ما، وتعميم الكلية بلا لحاظ خصوص الكمال فيعتق المكاتب أيضاً، لكن لا يصح قضاءً إخراج المدبّر وأمّ الولد؛ لأنه لا دلالة للفظ عليه، وفيه تخفيف له أيضاً، وفي إدخال المكاتب تشديد على نفسه وفي اللفظ أيضاً دلالة فيعتبر قضاء أيضاً، كذا في "الفصول".) (قوله: [لم يجز تصرفه فيه] أي: في المكاتب في البيع والهبة، ولو كان مملوكاً من كل وجه لجاز تصرفه، ومعتق البعض كالمكاتب عند الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله.) (قوله: [ولو تزوّج... آه] تفريع على السابق أي: عدم كون المكاتب مملوكاً له ملكاً تاماً، فإذا تزوّج بنت مولاه ثم مات مولاه وورثت زوجها المكاتب لأبيها، وملكته بحكم الإرث لم يفسد نكاحها معه حيث مات المولى وورثته البنت لم يفسد النكاح وإذا لم يكن مملوكاً من كل وجه لا يدخل تحت لفظ المملوك المطلق وهذا بخلاف المدبّر وأمّ الولد فإن الملك فيهما كامل ولذا حل وطئ المدبرة وأم الولد وإنّما النقصان في الرق من جهة التصرفات اللازمة. (قوله: [وإنّما النقصان... إلخ] جواب سوال مقدّر تقريره لما كان الملك كاملا يصح التحرير بهما الكفّارة مع أنه لا يصح بهما؛ لأنّ التدبير والاستيلاد تصرّفات لازمة لا تقبل الفسخ بحال فكان الرق فيهما ناقصاً فلا يصح أن يقع الكفّارة، ولقائل أن يقول: الأمر بالعكس وهو أن الملك في المكاتب كامل وفي المدبّر وأمّ الولد قاصر، بيانه أنّ العبد إذا كوتب لا يخرج عن ملك المولى، صرّح به الفقهاء لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «المكاتب عبد ما بقي عليه درهم ولهذا إذا عجز يكون مملوكاً كما كان، ولأنه إذا زال الملك بالكتابة لما عاد بالعجز كما في أم الولد والمدبّر، وإنما يخرج من يد المولى تحقيقاً لمعنى الكتابة وتحصيلاً للمقصود وهو أداء البدل، ولهذا يملك بالتصرفات ولا يملك المولى اكتسابه، وأمّا المدبّر وأمّ الولد فتطرق الخلل في ملكيتهما، ولهذا لا يقبلان العود إلى الملك كما كان، ولهذا لا يجوز بيعهما.)
Chapter on that by which literal meanings are abandoned. That is, abandoning the literal meaning due to an indication in the speech itself. Its example: if someone says, 'Every slave of mine is free,' his mukatabs (slaves who have made a contract of manumission) are not freed, nor are those who are partially freed, unless he intends their inclusion; because the word 'slave' is absolute and includes the slave from every aspect, while the mukatab is not a slave from every aspect. For this reason, he is not permitted to dispose of him, nor is it lawful for him to have intercourse with a female mukatab. If a mukatab marries his master's daughter, then the master dies and the daughter inherits the mukatab, the marriage does not become invalid. And since he is not a slave from every aspect, he does not fall under the absolute word 'slave.' This is contrary to the mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is deferred until the master's death) and the umm walad (a slave woman who has borne her master a child), for ownership over them is complete. For this reason, intercourse with a mudabbara and umm walad is lawful. The deficiency is only in the state of slavery due to irrevocable dispositions. Similarly, 'striking the Hatim with his garment' is literally, in language, absolute striking, but it has become known in common custom as a specific act of Hajj. Similarly, 'walking to the House of Allah' is absolute walking to it, even if intending to visit it, for Umrah, trade, or otherwise, but it has come to be used in custom to mean intending Hajj. (His saying: [Due to the existence of custom] For people intend by it the undertaking of Hajj, even if its linguistic meaning does not necessitate the obligation of Hajj. Thus, when the general word is intended for a specific meaning, it becomes a deficient literal meaning, not a metaphor. As stated in al-Ma'din.) (His saying: [Not freed... etc.] Because the abandoned literal meaning for the mukatab and the partially free is like a metaphor, so it does not become intended by the word except by intention. As stated in al-Fusul.) (His saying: [Unless he intends... etc.] That is, since the literal meaning—which is everything that has ownership even in some respect—is abandoned due to the establishment of the derivation root, rather the perfect descriptive attribute is intended from the indication of the absolute speech with the establishment of the complete root. Here, only the complete slave is intended unless he intends to include the incomplete one as well; because then it is like a metaphor or metonymy, requiring intention and resolve to include everything that has ownership in any way, and generalizing the totality without regard to the particular perfection, so the mukatab is also freed. But legally, the mudabbar and umm walad are not excluded [from the word 'slave'] because there is no indication in the word for them. There is also a relaxation for him (the speaker). Including the mukatab is a burden on himself, and the word also has an indication, so it is considered legally. As stated in al-Fusul.) (His saying: [He is not permitted to dispose of him] That is, concerning the mukatab in sale and gift. If he were a complete slave, his disposition would be permissible. And the partially free is like the mukatab according to Imam al-A'zam, may Allah have mercy on him.) (His saying: [If he marries... etc.] This is a branch of the preceding, i.e., the mukatab is not owned with complete ownership. So if he marries his master's daughter, then his master dies and the daughter inherits the mukatab, and becomes his owner by inheritance, her marriage with him does not become invalid. If the master dies and the daughter inherits him, the marriage does not become invalid, since he is not a complete slave, so he does not fall under the absolute word 'slave.' This is contrary to the mudabbar and umm walad, for ownership over them is complete. Therefore, intercourse with a mudabbara and umm walad is lawful; the deficiency is only in the state of slavery due to irrevocable dispositions. (His saying: [And the deficiency... etc.] An answer to an implied question: since ownership is complete, it would be valid to free them for expiation, but it is not valid with them because tadbir and istilad are irrevocable dispositions that cannot be rescinded at all, so the slavery in them is deficient, and thus they cannot be freed for expiation. Someone might say the opposite: that ownership in the mukatab is complete, while in the mudabbar and umm walad it is deficient. Explanation: when a slave enters a kitaba, he does not leave the master's ownership. The jurists explicitly state this due to the Prophet's saying: 'The mukatab is a slave as long as a dirham remains upon him.' Therefore, if he becomes unable, he becomes a slave as before. And if ownership were removed by the kitaba, it would not return due to inability, as in the case of umm walad and mudabbar. Rather, he leaves the master's hand to realize the meaning of kitaba and to achieve the objective of paying the substitute. For this reason, he has ownership of transactions, and the master does not own his earnings. As for the mudabbar and umm walad, a defect has entered their ownership; therefore they do not accept return to ownership as before, and for this reason they cannot be sold.)
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi explains the principle that the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word may be abandoned due to an internal indication (dalala fi nafs al-kalam) or due to custom (urf). The author provides several examples to illustrate when a general word ('amm) is used, but its literal scope is restricted by context or custom. The main example is the statement 'Every slave of mine is free.' The literal meaning of 'mamluk' (slave) includes anyone who is owned, even partially. However, due to common usage, the absolute word 'mamluk' is understood to refer to a complete slave (mamluk min kull wajh). Therefore, a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to earn his freedom) is not included, because he is not fully owned; ownership over him is incomplete. Similarly, a person who is partially free (mu'taq ba'd) is not included. However, if the speaker intends to include such individuals, his intention is effective because the abandoned literal meaning is akin to a metaphor, and intention is required to bring in the incomplete. The commentator notes that this is according to Abu Hanifa. The passage then discusses the case of a mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is deferred) and an umm walad (a slave mother of the master's child). These are fully owned, so they are included under 'mamluk' and intercourse with them is lawful. The deficiency in their case is only in the slavery aspect due to irrevocable dispositions (tasarrufat lazimah). The second example is the custom of 'striking the Hatim with one's garment' as a metaphor for performing Hajj. Although literally it means striking, in common custom it has come to refer to the act of Hajj. Similarly, 'walking to the House of Allah' is understood in custom as making Hajj, even though it could be for Umrah or trade. Thus, the passage demonstrates how custom (urf) can override the literal meaning and become a 'deficient literal meaning' (haqiqah qasirah) rather than a metaphor (majaz). The overall lesson is about the interaction between language and custom in legal interpretation, a key principle in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The original literal meaning of a word as established by language, which is abandoned due to contextual indication or custom.
مملوك (mamluk): A slave owned from every aspect (mamluk min kull wajh) as opposed to a mukatab or partially free slave.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitaba) to earn his freedom by paying a sum; he is not fully owned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone says 'every slave of mine is free,' which individuals are NOT freed unless the speaker specifically intends them?
A) A mudabbar and an umm walad
B) A mukatab and a partially free slave
C) A full slave and a mudabbar
D) An umm walad and a full slave

Key Takeaway
A general absolute word is understood in its complete literal meaning unless a contextual indication restricts it, requiring intention to include incomplete instances.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

Custom (Urf) as a Source for Abandoning Literal Meanings: The Hajj Examples
Usul rule with examples from Hajj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqah qasirah (deficient literal meaning) and majaz, and apply the Hanafi principle that custom can transform a general word into a specific customary meaning, as in 'striking the Hatim' and 'walking to the House of Allah'.

Core Idea
Custom creates a 'deficient literal meaning' that overrides the linguistic literal meaning, without becoming a metaphor.

In addition to contextual indications, common custom (urf) can also cause the literal meaning of a word or phrase to be abandoned. The author of Usul al-Shashi gives two examples from Hajj: 'striking the Hatim with his garment' and 'walking to the House of Allah.' While linguistically these are general actions, in customary usage they are understood as specific acts of Hajj. This transformation is unique because it produces a 'deficient literal meaning' (haqiqah qasirah) rather than a metaphor. In this lesson, you will learn how custom modifies meaning and why Hanafis classify it as a type of literal meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning
Take the phrase 'striking the Hatim with his garment.' Literally (lughatan), 'darb' means any kind of striking. 'Hatim' is a part of the Kaaba. So literally, the phrase means any act of striking that part.
This is the haqiqah lughawiyyah.
2
Recognize the custom
In the common custom (urf) of Muslims, this phrase is not understood as literal striking but as a specific action performed during Hajj: touching the Hatim or similar ritual. The custom is widespread and well-known.
Urf 'amm.
3
Classify the new meaning
Because of the custom, the phrase takes on a new meaning that is still considered a type of literal meaning (haqiqah) but 'deficient' (qasirah) because it is restricted to a specific usage. Hanafis distinguish this from majaz (metaphor).
Haqiqah qasirah: the original literal meaning is not completely abandoned; rather, the word is used in a specific sense that is the primary by custom.
4
Apply the principle to the second example
Similarly, 'walking to the House of Allah' literally means any walking to the Kaaba, even for trade or Umrah. But customarily, it is understood as intending Hajj. This is also a haqiqah qasirah due to urf.
Thus, custom can restrict the scope of a general phrase to a specific legal act.

(قوله: [وإنّما النقصان... إلخ] جواب سوال مقدّر تقريره لما كان الملك كاملا يصح التحرير بهما الكفّارة مع أنه لا يصح بهما؛ لأنّ التدبير والاستيلاد تصرّفات لازمة لا تقبل الفسخ بحال فكان الرق فيهما ناقصاً فلا يصح أن يقع الكفّارة، ولقائل أن يقول: الأمر بالعكس وهو أن الملك في المكاتب كامل وفي المدبّر وأمّ الولد قاصر، بيانه أنّ العبد إذا كوتب لا يخرج عن ملك المولى، صرّح به الفقهاء لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «المكاتب عبد ما بقي عليه درهم ولهذا إذا عجز يكون مملوكاً كما كان، ولأنه إذا زال الملك بالكتابة لما عاد بالعجز كما في أم الولد والمدبّر، وإنما يخرج من يد المولى تحقيقاً لمعنى الكتابة وتحصيلاً للمقصود وهو أداء البدل، ولهذا يملك بالتصرفات ولا يملك المولى اكتسابه، وأمّا المدبّر وأمّ الولد فتطرق الخلل في ملكيتهما، ولهذا لا يقبلان العود إلى الملك كما كان، ولهذا لا يجوز بيعهما.)

(His saying: [Due to the existence of custom] For people intend by it the undertaking of Hajj, even if its linguistic meaning does not necessitate the obligation of Hajj. Thus, when the general word is intended for a specific meaning, it becomes a deficient literal meaning, not a metaphor. As stated in al-Ma'din.) (His saying: [Not freed... etc.] Because the abandoned literal meaning for the mukatab and the partially free is like a metaphor, so it does not become intended by the word except by intention. As stated in al-Fusul.) (His saying: [Unless he intends... etc.] That is, since the literal meaning—which is everything that has ownership even in some respect—is abandoned due to the establishment of the derivation root, rather the perfect descriptive attribute is intended from the indication of the absolute speech with the establishment of the complete root. Here, only the complete slave is intended unless he intends to include the incomplete one as well; because then it is like a metaphor or metonymy, requiring intention and resolve to include everything that has ownership in any way, and generalizing the totality without regard to the particular perfection, so the mukatab is also freed. But legally, the mudabbar and umm walad are not excluded [from the word 'slave'] because there is no indication in the word for them. There is also a relaxation for him (the speaker). Including the mukatab is a burden on himself, and the word also has an indication, so it is considered legally. As stated in al-Fusul.) (His saying: [He is not permitted to dispose of him] That is, concerning the mukatab in sale and gift. If he were a complete slave, his disposition would be permissible. And the partially free is like the mukatab according to Imam al-A'zam, may Allah have mercy on him.) (His saying: [If he marries... etc.] This is a branch of the preceding, i.e., the mukatab is not owned with complete ownership. So if he marries his master's daughter, then his master dies and the daughter inherits the mukatab, and becomes his owner by inheritance, her marriage with him does not become invalid. If the master dies and the daughter inherits him, the marriage does not become invalid, since he is not a complete slave, so he does not fall under the absolute word 'slave.' This is contrary to the mudabbar and umm walad, for ownership over them is complete. Therefore, intercourse with a mudabbara and umm walad is lawful; the deficiency is only in the state of slavery due to irrevocable dispositions. (His saying: [And the deficiency... etc.] An answer to an implied question: since ownership is complete, it would be valid to free them for expiation, but it is not valid with them because tadbir and istilad are irrevocable dispositions that cannot be rescinded at all, so the slavery in them is deficient, and thus they cannot be freed for expiation. Someone might say the opposite: that ownership in the mukatab is complete, while in the mudabbar and umm walad it is deficient. Explanation: when a slave enters a kitaba, he does not leave the master's ownership. The jurists explicitly state this due to the Prophet's saying: 'The mukatab is a slave as long as a dirham remains upon him.' Therefore, if he becomes unable, he becomes a slave as before. And if ownership were removed by the kitaba, it would not return due to inability, as in the case of umm walad and mudabbar. Rather, he leaves the master's hand to realize the meaning of kitaba and to achieve the objective of paying the substitute. For this reason, he has ownership of transactions, and the master does not own his earnings. As for the mudabbar and umm walad, a defect has entered their ownership; therefore they do not accept return to ownership as before, and for this reason they cannot be sold.)
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Shashi explains the principle that the literal meaning (haqiqah) of a word may be abandoned due to an internal indication (dalala fi nafs al-kalam) or due to custom (urf). The author provides several examples to illustrate when a general word ('amm) is used, but its literal scope is restricted by context or custom. The main example is the statement 'Every slave of mine is free.' The literal meaning of 'mamluk' (slave) includes anyone who is owned, even partially. However, due to common usage, the absolute word 'mamluk' is understood to refer to a complete slave (mamluk min kull wajh). Therefore, a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to earn his freedom) is not included, because he is not fully owned; ownership over him is incomplete. Similarly, a person who is partially free (mu'taq ba'd) is not included. However, if the speaker intends to include such individuals, his intention is effective because the abandoned literal meaning is akin to a metaphor, and intention is required to bring in the incomplete. The commentator notes that this is according to Abu Hanifa. The passage then discusses the case of a mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is deferred) and an umm walad (a slave mother of the master's child). These are fully owned, so they are included under 'mamluk' and intercourse with them is lawful. The deficiency in their case is only in the slavery aspect due to irrevocable dispositions (tasarrufat lazimah). The second example is the custom of 'striking the Hatim with one's garment' as a metaphor for performing Hajj. Although literally it means striking, in common custom it has come to refer to the act of Hajj. Similarly, 'walking to the House of Allah' is understood in custom as making Hajj, even though it could be for Umrah or trade. Thus, the passage demonstrates how custom (urf) can override the literal meaning and become a 'deficient literal meaning' (haqiqah qasirah) rather than a metaphor (majaz). The overall lesson is about the interaction between language and custom in legal interpretation, a key principle in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
حقيقة قاصرة (haqiqah qasirah): A deficient literal meaning: a word's meaning that is restricted to a specific instance due to custom, still considered literal rather than metaphorical.
عرف (urf): Common custom that overrides the literal meaning, making a specific usage a 'haqiqah urfiyyah' (customary literal meaning).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the passage, the phrase 'striking the Hatim with his garment' is classified as:
A) Majaz (metaphor) because it is not literal striking.
B) Haqiqah qasirah (deficient literal meaning) due to custom.
C) Haqiqah lughawiyyah (linguistic literal meaning) without change.
D) Mujmal (ambiguous) because it can mean different things.

Key Takeaway
Custom can create a deficient literal meaning (haqiqah qasirah) that overrides the linguistic literal meaning, distinct from metaphor.

The Rule of Abandoning Literal Meaning Due to Context in Hanafi Usul
Stating the usul rule of abandoning literal meaning via context, with aman examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when siyaq al-kalam overrides haqiqah and apply this Hanafi principle to new examples from aman rulings.

Core Idea
Context can suspend the literal meaning — siyaq al-kalam is a decisive indicator in Hanafi usul.

In Hanafi usul, the literal meaning (haqiqah) is the default, but it yields to contextual clues (siyaq al-kalam). The third method of abandoning haqiqah is through the context of speech. This lesson examines examples from Imam Muhammad's al-Siyar al-Kabir regarding safe conduct (aman) for enemy combatants (harbi). These scenarios demonstrate how context transforms a simple command into an offer of safety or a threat, changing the legal outcome. By analyzing these cases, you will learn how to apply this usul principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning
For each statement, determine the haqiqah (literal meaning) of the words used. For example, 'descent' literally means to come down.
The literal meaning is what the word signifies in linguistic convention.
2
Examine the context
Look at the full phrase and the circumstances. Is there any conditional phrase (e.g., 'if you are a man') or additional speech that changes the tone?
Context includes both verbal context and the situation.
3
Determine if context overrides haqiqah
Does the context clearly indicate a meaning different from the literal? If yes, the literal is abandoned and the contextual meaning takes effect.
If context is ambiguous, haqiqah remains.
4
Apply the usul principle
State the rule: when siyaq al-kalam clearly contradicts haqiqah, the latter is abandoned. This is one of the methods of ta'wil.
This principle is used in interpreting Qur'an and Hadith as well.
5
Derive the legal consequence
Based on the contextual meaning, determine the ruling. In the aman examples, the consequence is whether the harbi becomes safe or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يترك به حقائق الألفاظ. والثالث: قد تترك الحقيقة بدلالة سياق الكلام. قال في "السير الكبير": إذا قال المسلم للحربي: "إنزل" فنزل كان آمناً. ولو قال: "إنزل إن كنت رجلاً" فنزل لا يكون آمناً. ولو قال الحربي: "الأمان الأمان" فقال المسلم: "الأمان الأمان" كان أمناً. ولو قال: "الأمان ستعلم ما تلقى غداً ولا تعجل حتى ترى" فنزل لا يكون آمناً. وفيه العمل بالحقيقة.
Chapter on what causes the literal meanings of words to be abandoned. The third: The literal meaning may be abandoned by indication of the context of speech. He said in 'al-Siyar al-Kabir': If a Muslim says to a harbi: 'Descend,' and he descends, he is safe. And if he says: 'Descend if you are a man,' and he descends, he is not safe. And if the harbi says: 'Safety, safety,' and the Muslim says: 'Safety, safety,' he is safe. And if he says: 'Safety, you will know what you will receive tomorrow, and do not hurry until you see,' and he descends, he is not safe. And in it is acting upon the literal meaning.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage states the third method by which literal meanings are abandoned: contextual clues (siyaq al-kalam). The author cites examples from Imam Muhammad's al-Siyar al-Kabir regarding interactions with enemy combatants (harbi). The word 'descend' is literally a command, but when said without condition, context indicates an offer of aman (safe conduct). Thus the literal command is reinterpreted as permission. However, when the Muslim adds 'if you are a man,' the context becomes threatening (implying 'if you have courage, come down and fight'), so the descent does not grant aman. Similarly, the harbi's repetition of 'al-aman' is a request, and the Muslim's identical reply grants it by context. But if the Muslim responds with a threat ('you will know what you will receive'), the context negates aman. The phrase 'wa fihi al-amal bil-haqiqah' indicates that in the latter case, the literal meaning of the words (as threat) is upheld.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The literal, primary meaning of a word as per linguistic convention.
سياق الكلام (siyaq al-kalam): The verbal context or flow of speech that indicates the intended meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a Muslim tells a harbi 'Descend if you are a man' and the harbi descends, what is the ruling regarding his safety?
A) He becomes safe because he obeyed the command.
B) He does not become safe because the context indicates a threat.
C) He becomes safe only if he says 'aman' in return.
D) His safety depends on the Muslim's intention.

Key Takeaway
Context (siyaq al-kalam) can override the literal meaning of words; the interpreter must consider the full speech situation.

Contextual Restriction in Purchases and the Fly Hadith
Applying context to restrict absolute terms in agency and the fly hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contextual evidence (qarinah) that restricts an absolute term or overrides the literal meaning of a command, and apply the Hanafi rule of taqyid bi al-siyaq to derive the correct ruling.

Core Idea
Context can restrict absolute terms and override literal commands—the purpose determines the legal effect.

In this lesson, we examine two classic Hanafi examples where the literal meaning of a word or command is abandoned due to contextual evidence. The first involves an agent buying a slave girl; the second is the well‑known hadith of the fly. Both illustrate how the Hanafi school gives weight to qarinah (contextual indication) when interpreting texts, a principle rooted in the school's emphasis on purpose and custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal term or command
Locate the key phrase in each example: 'jariyah' (slave girl) in the agency case, and 'imquluhu' (dip it) in the fly hadith.
These are the linguistic elements that carry a default legal meaning.
2
Determine the default Hanafi ruling
For the agency, the absolute term 'jariyah' includes all female slaves; for the command, the imperative indicates obligation (wujub).
This is the asl (original rule) before considering context.
3
Search for contextual evidence (qarinah)
In the agency case, the phrase 'to serve me' or 'so that I may have intercourse with her' provides a purpose. In the hadith, the phrase 'in one wing is disease, in the other is cure' indicates a remedy.
The qarinah must be explicit or strongly implied in the speech.
4
Apply taqyid (restriction) or sarf (diversion)
The context restricts the absolute term to slave girls fit for service or lawful intercourse; the command is diverted from obligation to recommendation because the purpose is worldly benefit.
Hanafi usul holds that a qarinah of purpose overrides literal meaning.
5
State the resulting ruling
For agency: the purchase is not valid for the principal. For the fly: dipping is mustahabb, not wajib.
Opposing schools (e.g., Zahiri) who reject such qarinah would rule differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: اشتر لي جارية لتخدمني فاشترى العمياء أو الشلاء لا يجوز. ولو قال: اشتر لي جارية حتى أطاها فاشترى أخته من الرضاع لا يكون عن الموكل. وعلى هذا قلنا في قوله عليه السلام: «إذا وقع الذباب في طعام أحدكم فامقلوه ثم انقلوه فإن في إحدى جناحيه داء وفي الأخرى دواء» فإن حقيقة قوله «فامقلوه» هي وجوب المقل قضية للأمر، لكن تركت حقيقة بسياق الكلام؛ لأنه دل على أن المقل لدفع الداء. قوله: فامقلوه فإن حقيقة الأمر الوجوب لكن تركت بدلالة سياق الكلام.
And if he says: 'Buy me a slave girl to serve me,' and he bought a blind or one-armed girl, it is not permissible. And if he says: 'Buy me a slave girl so that I may have intercourse with her,' and he bought his sister from fosterage, it is not on behalf of the principal. And upon this we said regarding his statement, peace be upon him: 'When a fly falls into the food of any of you, then dip it completely and then remove it, for in one of its wings is disease and in the other is cure.' For the literal meaning of his statement 'dip it' is the obligation of dipping as required by the command, but its literal meaning is abandoned due to context of speech, because it indicates that dipping is to repel the disease. His statement: 'dip it' – the literal meaning of the command is obligation, but it is abandoned by indication of context.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage provides two more examples of abandoning literal meaning due to context. The first is from agency (wakalah): when a principal says 'buy a slave girl to serve me,' the absolute word 'jariyah' is contextually restricted to one fit for service; a blind or one-armed girl does not fulfill the purpose. Similarly, 'so that I may have intercourse with her' restricts to a lawful partner, so buying a foster sister (who is unlawful for marriage) is invalid. The second example is the famous hadith of the fly. The command 'dip it' (imquluhu) literally indicates obligation (wujub), but the context—the mention of disease and cure in the wings—shows the purpose is to obtain the cure, so the command is interpreted as recommendation (nadb) or permissibility, not obligation. This is a clear instance of abandoning the literal meaning of an imperative due to contextual evidence.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): An absolute, unrestricted term.
تقييد (taqyid): Restriction of a general term by a qualifying condition or context.
أمر (amr): A command verb; its literal implication is obligation (wujub) unless contextual evidence overrides.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the command 'dip it' in the fly hadith not considered obligatory?
A) Because the fly is impure.
B) Because the context shows the purpose is to obtain the cure, not to perform an obligatory act.
C) Because the hadith is weak.
D) Because it was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Contextual evidence (qarinah siyaqiyyah) can restrict absolute terms and override the literal meaning of commands, a key Hanafi tool for deriving rulings that align with the Lawgiver's intent.

Abandoning Literal Meanings Due to Context (Siyaq)
Introduction to abandoning haqiqah for siyaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of context (siyaq) in overriding literal meanings (haqiqah) and apply this principle to interpret Quranic verses and prophetic hadith in accordance with Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Context (siyaq) overrides literal meaning when it reveals the speaker's true intent.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, words are understood according to their literal meanings (haqiqah) unless a contextual indicator (qarinah) shows that the speaker intended something else. This lesson explores the chapter from Usul al-Shashi that illustrates this principle through four examples from fiqh: the granting of aman, buying a slave girl, the fly hadith, and the zakat verse. By studying these, you will learn how to recognize when context transforms a command from obligation to recommendation, or from general to specific.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal meaning being abandoned
For each example, pinpoint the word or phrase whose apparent literal meaning is set aside. What does the word ordinarily signify?
E.g., in the fly hadith, the imperative 'faimquluhu' literally indicates obligation.
2
Locate the contextual indicator (qarinah)
Look at the surrounding words, the situation, or the broader text that signals the speaker's actual intent. This could be a purpose clause, a preceding verse, or customary usage.
In the fly hadith, the context of disease and cure indicates the purpose is benefit, not obligation.
3
Determine the resulting legal ruling
Based on the contextual indicator, what is the final hukm? Does the command become recommended? Does the general become restricted?
For the fly hadith, the ruling changes from wajib to mandub.
4
Articulate the usul principle
State the principle that governs this operation: 'Al-haqiqah tutrak bi al-siyaq' or similar. This principle is a key tool for istinbat.
This principle is rooted in the Hanafi emphasis on the speaker's intent as understood from context.
5
Compare with other madhahib (if applicable)
For the zakat verse, note the Shafi'i counter-argument. Recognize that the same textual evidence can lead to different rulings depending on the weight given to context.
This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of differing usul approaches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يترك به حقائق الألفاظ. لا يكون آمنا ولو قال الحربي: الأمان الأمان فقال المسلم: «الأمان الأمان» كان أمنا ولو قال: «الأمان ستعلم ما تلقى غداً ولا تعجل حتى ترى» لم يكن آمنا. ولو قال رجل لرجل: اشتر لي جارية لتخدمني فاشترى العمياء أو الشلاء لا يجوز. ولو قال: اشتر لي جارية حتى أطأها فاشترى أخته من الرضاع لا يكون عن الموكل. وعلى هذا قلنا في قوله عليه السلام: «إذا وقع الذباب في طعام أحدكم فامقلوه ثم انقلوه فإن في إحدى جناحيه داء وفي الأخرى دواء» فإنه وإن كان حقيقة الأمر الوجوب لكن تركت حقيقة بسياق الكلام. وأيضا قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّمَا الصَّدَقَاتُ لِلْفُقَرَاءِ وَالْمَسَاكِينِ ...﴾ [التوبة: 60] عقيب قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمِنْهُم مَّن يَلْمِزُكَ فِي الصَّدَقَاتِ﴾ [التوبة: 58] يدل على أن ذكر الأصناف لقطع طمع المنافقين لا للاستحقاق. ومعنى قوله «ستعلم ما تلقى غداً» في العرف التهديد والتوبيخ، فصار مجازاً. وقوله «لا يكون عن المؤكل» لأن حقيقة الكلام متروكة لسياق الكلام؛ لأنه علم بقوله «لتخدمني» أو «حتى أطأها» مراده. وقوله «فامقلوه» الأمر بالمقل لدفع الأذى لا للإيجاب، لأنه نفع لنا لا تكليف محض. وقوله تعالى في سياق الصدقات يدل على أن ذكر الأصناف لبيان المصرف لا لاستحقاق كل فرد.
Chapter on what causes the literal meanings of words to be abandoned. It is not aman if the Harbi says: al-aman al-aman and the Muslim says: al-aman al-aman, it is aman. If he says: al-aman you will know tomorrow what you will meet and do not hasten until you see, he is not aman. If a man says to a man: buy for me a slave girl to serve me, and he buys a blind or one-armed one, it is not valid. If he says: buy for me a slave girl so that I may have intercourse with her, and he buys his foster sister, it is not binding on the principal. And based on this we said regarding his statement peace be upon him: if a fly falls into the food of one of you, dip it and then remove it, for in one of its wings there is disease and in the other cure; although the literal meaning of the command is obligation, its literal meaning was abandoned due to the context of the speech. And also the statement of Allah Most High: 'Verily the charities are for the poor...' [9:60] after His statement: 'And among them are those who criticize you concerning the charities' [9:58] indicates that the mention of the categories is to cut off the hope of the hypocrites, not for entitlement. And the meaning of his statement 'you will know tomorrow what you will meet' in custom is threat and rebuke, so it became metaphorical. And his statement 'it is not binding on the principal' because the literal meaning of the speech is abandoned due to the context of the speech; because it was known from his statement 'to serve me' or 'so that I may have intercourse with her' his intent. And his statement 'dip it' the command to dip is for repelling harm, not for obligation, because it is a benefit for us, not a pure obligation. And the statement of Allah Most High in the context of charities indicates that the mention of the categories is to explain the channels of disbursement, not for entitlement of every individual.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on the abandonment of literal meanings (haqiqah) of words in favor of contextual (siyaq) or customary indications. The author presents four illustrative cases from Hanafi fiqh. First, a Harbi seeking aman by repeating 'al-aman' is granted aman if the Muslim responds likewise, but if the reply is a threat ('you will know tomorrow'), the literal meaning of 'al-aman' is abandoned and no aman is established. Second, when someone instructs to buy a slave girl for service, the literal command is restricted by context to a girl fit for service; buying a blind or one-armed girl does not fulfill the intent. Similarly, buying a foster sister for intercourse is invalid because the context specified a lawful partner. Third, the hadith about a fly falling into food commands dipping it, but the context—that one wing has disease and the other cure—shows the command is for benefit, not obligation (it is a recommendation). Fourth, the verse on the eight categories of zakat recipients (9:60), when read after the verse criticizing those who dispute distribution (9:58), indicates the categories are for disbursement, not that each individual is entitled. The commentary (from Ahsan al-Hawashi) explains that in each case, the literal meaning is abandoned due to a contextual indicator (siyaq al-kalam), such as a threat in speech, a purpose clause, or a surrounding verse. This principle is central to Hanafi usul: the siyaq can qualify or override the apparent meaning.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as established by linguistic convention.
سياق الكلام (siyaq al-kalam): The context or flow of speech that indicates the intended meaning of a word or phrase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of buying a slave girl for service ('li-takhdumani'), why is purchasing a blind or one-armed slave girl invalid?
A) Because such slave girls are generally not available for purchase.
B) Because the literal command is general and must be fulfilled as stated.
C) Because the context (purpose of service) restricts the command to a slave girl fit for service.
D) Because the buyer's speech is invalid due to ignorance.

Key Takeaway
Context (siyaq) is a powerful indicator that can override the literal meaning of words, a cornerstone of Hanafi textual interpretation.
